</div>
<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LLNLUSEnergy2011.png"><img alt="Estimated US Energy Use/Flow in 2011. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #3fc041; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Biomass</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #006000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Petroleum</div>

<td colspan="3" style=""><i>Producing Electrical Currents/Utilizing Effects Transmitted</i></td>

<tr>
<td colspan="3" valign="top">
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #ffd37c; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Electricity generation</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #fecccb; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Residential, Commercial, Industrial, transportation</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #b9b9b9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Rejected energy (waste heat)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #f2f2f2; background-color: #616161; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span>  Energy services</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Energy development</b> is the field of activities focused on obtaining sources of energy from natural resources. These activities include production of conventional, <a href="/wiki/Alternative_energy" title="Alternative energy">alternative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable</a> sources of energy, and for the <a href="/wiki/Waste_heat" title="Waste heat">recovery and reuse of energy</a> that would otherwise be wasted. <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">efficiency measures</a> reduce the demand for energy development, and can have benefits to society with improvements to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issue" title="Environmental issue">environmental issues</a>.</p>
<p>Societies use energy for transportation, manufacturing, illumination, heating and air conditioning, and communication, for industrial, commercial, and domestic purposes. Energy resources may be classified as primary resources, where the resource can be used in substantially its original form, or as secondary resources, where the energy source must be converted into a more conveniently usable form. Non-renewable resources are significantly depleted by human use, whereas renewable resources are produced by ongoing processes that can sustain indefinite human exploitation.</p>
<p>Thousands of people are employed in the <a href="/wiki/Energy_industry" title="Energy industry">energy industry</a>. The conventional industry comprises the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">petroleum industry</a>, the natural gas industry, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" title="Electrical power industry">electrical power industry</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_industry" title="Nuclear industry">nuclear industry</a>. New energy industries include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_industry" title="Renewable energy industry">renewable energy industry</a>, comprising alternative and sustainable manufacture, distribution, and sale of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuels</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_resources">Classification of resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification of resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" title="World energy resources and consumption">World energy resources and consumption</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_energy_supply" title="Worldwide energy supply">Worldwide energy supply</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_and_society" title="Energy and society">Energy and society</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_planning" title="Energy planning">Energy planning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy" title="Energy policy">Energy policy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basic_Open_System_Model.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="618" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Basic_Open_System_Model.gif/220px-Basic_Open_System_Model.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Basic_Open_System_Model.gif/330px-Basic_Open_System_Model.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Basic_Open_System_Model.gif/440px-Basic_Open_System_Model.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basic_Open_System_Model.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Open System Model (basics)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Energy resources may be classified as primary resources, suitable for end use without conversion to another form, or secondary resources, where the usable form of energy required substantial conversion from a primary source. Examples of primary energy resources are <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, wood fuel, fossil fuels such as coal, oil and natural gas, and uranium. Secondary resources are those such as electricity, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen</a>, or other synthetic fuels.</p>
<p>Another important classification is based on the time required to regenerate an energy resource. "Renewable" resources are those that recover their capacity in a time significant by human needs. Examples are hydroelectric power or wind power, when the natural phenomena that are the primary source of energy are ongoing and not depleted by human demands. Non-renewable resources are those that are significantly depleted by human usage and that will not recover their potential significantly during human lifetimes. An example of a non-renewable energy source is coal, which does not form naturally at a rate that would support human use.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuels">Fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg/220px-Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg/330px-Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg/440px-Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moss_Landing_Power_Plant_p1270026.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Moss_Landing_Power_Plant" title="Moss Landing Power Plant">Moss Landing Power Plant</a> in California is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil-fuel power station</a> that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_gas_power_plant" title="Natural gas power plant">burns natural gas in a turbine</a> to produce electricity</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">Peak oil</a></div>
<p>Fossil fuel (<i>primary non-renewable fossil</i>) sources burn <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fuels, which are the remains of the decomposition of plants and animals. There are three main types of fossil fuels: coal, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. Another fossil fuel, <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> (LPG), is principally derived from the production of natural gas. Heat from burning fossil fuel is used either directly for space heating and process heating, or converted to mechanical energy for vehicles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_process" title="Industrial process">industrial processes</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_generation" title="Electrical power generation">electrical power generation</a>. These fossil fuels are part of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> and thus allow stored solar energy to be used today.</p>
<p>The use of fossil fuels in the 18th and 19th Century set the stage for the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.</p>
<p>Fossil fuels make up the bulk of the world's current <a href="/wiki/Primary_energy" title="Primary energy">primary energy</a> sources. In 2005, 81% of the world's energy needs was met from fossil sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The technology and infrastructure already exist for the use of fossil fuels. Liquid fuels derived from petroleum deliver a great deal of usable energy per unit of weight or volume, which is advantageous when compared with lower <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> sources such as a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a>. Fossil fuels are currently economical for decentralised energy use.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="1872" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/190px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/285px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg/380px-BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BarnettShaleDrilling-9323.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horizontal_drilling" title="Horizontal drilling">horizontal</a>) <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rig</a> for <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> in Texas</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_dependence" title="Energy dependence">Energy dependence</a> on imported fossil fuels creates <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a> risks for dependent countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Oil dependence in particular has led to war,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> funding of radicals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> monopolization,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and socio-political instability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fossil fuels are non-renewable resources, which will eventually decline in production <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and become exhausted. While the processes that created fossil fuels are ongoing, fuels are consumed far more quickly than the natural rate of replenishment. Extracting fuels becomes increasingly costly as society consumes the most accessible fuel deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Extraction of fossil fuels results in <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strip_mining" title="Strip mining">strip mining</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountaintop_removal" title="Mountaintop removal">mountaintop removal</a> of coal.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">Fuel efficiency</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">thermal efficiency</a>, meaning the efficiency of a process that converts chemical potential energy contained in a carrier <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> into <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">work</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> is the energy efficiency of a particular vehicle, is given as a <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> of distance travelled per unit of <a href="/wiki/Motor_fuel" title="Motor fuel">fuel</a> consumed. Weight-specific efficiency (efficiency per unit weight) may be stated for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freight" title="Freight">freight</a>, and passenger-specific efficiency (vehicle efficiency per passenger). The inefficient atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> (burning) of fossil fuels in vehicles, buildings, and power plants contributes to <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conventional production of oil has <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peaked</a>, conservatively, between 2007 and 2010. In 2010, it was estimated that an investment in non-renewable resources of $8 trillion would be required to maintain current levels of production for 25 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In 2010, governments subsidized <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> by an estimated $500 billion a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily1_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily1-18">[18]</a></sup> Fossil fuels are also a source of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions, leading to concerns about <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> if consumption is not reduced.</p>
<p>The combustion of fossil fuels leads to the release of <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> into the atmosphere. The fossil fuels are mainly carbon compounds. During <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> is released, and also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxides" title="Nitrogen oxides">nitrogen oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> and other fine <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particulates</a>. Man-made carbon dioxide according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a> contributes to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Other emissions from fossil fuel power station include <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a> (VOC), <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> including traces of <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>A typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coal_plant" title="Coal plant">coal plant</a> generates billions of <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hour" title="Kilowatt hour">kilowatt hours</a> per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear">Nuclear</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nuclear">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission">Fission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Fission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="679" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg/225px-Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg/338px-Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg/450px-Susquehanna_steam_electric_station.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Susquehanna_Steam_Electric_Station" title="Susquehanna Steam Electric Station">Susquehanna Steam Electric Station</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Boiling_water_reactor" title="Boiling water reactor">boiling water reactor</a>. The reactors are located inside the rectangular <a href="/wiki/Containment_building" title="Containment building">containment buildings</a> towards the front of the <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling towers</a>. The power station produces 63 million <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt_hour" title="Kilowatt hour">kilowatt hours</a> per day.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Enterprise_(CVAN-65),_USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)_and_USS_Bainbridge_(DLGN-25)_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit,_in_1964.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="1062" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/225px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/338px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg/450px-USS_Enterprise_%28CVAN-65%29%2C_USS_Long_Beach_%28CGN-9%29_and_USS_Bainbridge_%28DLGN-25%29_underway_in_the_Mediterranean_Sea_during_Operation_Sea_Orbit%2C_in_1964.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">American nuclear powered ships,(top to bottom) cruisers <a href="/wiki/USS_Bainbridge_(CGN-25)" title="USS Bainbridge (CGN-25)">USS <i>Bainbridge</i></a>, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Long_Beach_(CGN-9)" title="USS Long Beach (CGN-9)">USS <i>Long Beach</i></a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)">USS Enterprise</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_longest_naval_ships" title="List of longest naval ships">longest ever naval vessel</a>, and the first nuclear-powered <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carrier</a>. Picture taken in 1964 during a record setting voyage of 26,540 nmi (49,190 km) around the world in 65 days without refueling. Crew members are spelling out <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass-energy equivalence</a> formula <i>E = mc<sup>2</sup></i> on the flight deck.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="245" data-file-width="420" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg/225px-NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg/338px-NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/NSF_picture_of_Yamal.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Russian <a href="/wiki/Nuclear-powered_icebreaker" title="Nuclear-powered icebreaker">nuclear-powered icebreaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Yamal_(icebreaker)" title="Yamal (icebreaker)">NS Yamal</a> on a joint scientific expedition with the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">NSF</a> in 1994</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">Nuclear power</a> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> to generate useful <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. Fission of uranium produces nearly all economically significant nuclear power. <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">Radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> form a very small component of energy generation, mostly in specialized applications such as deep space vehicles.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plants</a>, excluding <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_marine_propulsion" title="Nuclear marine propulsion">naval reactors</a>, provided about 5.7% of the world's energy and 13% of the world's electricity in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a> report that there are 437 operational nuclear power reactors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea.org_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaea.org-24">[24]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_by_country" title="Nuclear power by country">31 countries</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UIC_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-UIC-25">[25]</a></sup> although not every reactor is producing electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In addition, there are approximately 140 naval vessels using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_propulsion" title="Nuclear propulsion">nuclear propulsion</a> in operation, powered by some 180 reactors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> As of 2013, attaining a <a href="/wiki/Joint_European_Torus" title="Joint European Torus">net energy gain</a> from sustained nuclear fusion reactions, excluding natural fusion power sources such as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, remains an ongoing area of international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Thermonuclear_Experimental_Reactor" title="International Thermonuclear Experimental Reactor">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Fusion_Materials_Irradiation_Facility" title="International Fusion Materials Irradiation Facility">engineering research</a>. More than 60 years after the first attempts, commercial fusion power production remains unlikely before 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ITERorg_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ITERorg-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is an ongoing <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_debate" title="Nuclear power debate">debate about nuclear power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstor.org_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_Reasonable_Bet_on_Nuclear_Power-33">[33]</a></sup> Proponents, such as the <a href="/wiki/World_Nuclear_Association" title="World Nuclear Association">World Nuclear Association</a>, the <a href="/wiki/International_Atomic_Energy_Agency" title="International Atomic Energy Agency">IAEA</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmentalists_for_Nuclear_Energy" title="Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy">Environmentalists for Nuclear Energy</a> contend that nuclear power is a safe, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">sustainable energy</a> source that reduces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_emissions" title="Carbon emissions">carbon emissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomberg.com_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomberg.com-34">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement" title="Anti-nuclear movement">Opponents</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenpeace_International" title="Greenpeace International">Greenpeace International</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Information_and_Resource_Service" title="Nuclear Information and Resource Service">NIRS</a>, contend that nuclear power poses many threats to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity" title="Environmental radioactivity">people and the environment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Share_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Share-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NC_WARN_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-NC_WARN-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturgis_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturgis-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">Nuclear power plant accidents</a> include the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a> (1986), <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> (2011), and the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island accident</a> (1979).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-38">[38]</a></sup> There have also been some nuclear submarine accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timenuke_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-timenuke-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rad_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-rad-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-johnston2007_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-johnston2007-40">[40]</a></sup> In terms of lives lost per unit of energy generated, analysis has determined that nuclear power has caused less fatalities per unit of energy generated than the other major sources of energy generation. Energy production from <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> has caused a greater number of fatalities per unit of energy generated due to <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_accidents" title="Energy accidents">energy accident</a> effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2007_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2007-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-without_the_hot_air_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-without_the_hot_air-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theage2006_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-theage2006-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tandfonline1_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline1-45">[45]</a></sup> However, the economic costs of nuclear power accidents is high, and meltdowns can take decades to clean up. The human costs of evacuations of affected populations and lost livelihoods is also significant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richard_Schiffman_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richard_Schiffman-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Fackler_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Fackler-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comparing Nuclear's <i>latent</i> cancer deaths, such as cancer with other energy sources <i>immediate</i> deaths per unit of energy generated(GWeyr). This study does not include fossil fuel related cancer and other indirect deaths created by the use of fossil fuel consumption in its "severe accident" classification, which would be an accident with more than 5 fatalities.</p>
<p>Nuclear power is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_carbon_power_generation" title="Low carbon power generation">low carbon power generation</a> method of producing electricity, with an analysis of the literature on its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" title="Life cycle assessment">total life cycle</a> <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">emission intensity</a> finding that it is similar to renewable sources in a comparison of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>(GHG) emissions per unit of energy generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Since the 1970s, nuclear fuel has displaced about 64 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigaton" title="Gigaton">gigatonnes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_equivalent" title="Carbon dioxide equivalent">carbon dioxide equivalent</a>(GtCO2-eq) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a>, that would have otherwise resulted from the burning of oil, coal or natural gas in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil-fuel_power_station" title="Fossil-fuel power station">fossil-fuel power stations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2012, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAEA" title="IAEA">IAEA</a>, worldwide there were 68 civil nuclear power reactors under construction in 15 countries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaea.org_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaea.org-24">[24]</a></sup> approximately 28 of which in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peoples_Republic_of_China" title="Peoples Republic of China">Peoples Republic of China</a> (PRC), with the most recent nuclear power reactor, as of May 2013, to be connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>, occurring on February 17, 2013 in <a href="/wiki/Hongyanhe_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Hongyanhe Nuclear Power Plant">Hongyanhe Nuclear Power Plant</a> in the PRC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> In the United States, two new <a href="/wiki/Generation_III_reactor" title="Generation III reactor">Generation III reactors</a> are under construction at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vogtle" title="Vogtle">Vogtle</a>. U.S. nuclear industry officials expect five new reactors to enter service by 2020, all at existing plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us12_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-us12-51">[51]</a></sup> In 2013, four aging, uncompetitive, reactors were permanently closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mark_Cooper_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_Cooper-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mw11111_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-mw11111-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan's 2011 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_accident" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear accident</a>, which occurred in a reactor design from the <a href="/wiki/Generation_II_reactor" title="Generation II reactor">1960s</a>, prompted a rethink of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_safety" title="Nuclear safety">nuclear safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_energy_policy" title="Nuclear energy policy">nuclear energy policy</a> in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-54">[54]</a></sup> Germany decided to close all its reactors by 2022, and Italy has banned nuclear power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciamer2011_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciamer2011-54">[54]</a></sup> Following Fukushima, in 2011 the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> halved its estimate of additional nuclear generating capacity to be built by 2035.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economist-20110428_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-economist-20110428-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-late_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-late-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recent experiments in extraction of uranium use polymer ropes that are coated with a substance that selectively absorbs uranium from seawater. This process could make the considerable volume of uranium dissolved in seawater exploitable for energy production. Since ongoing geologic processes carry uranium to the sea in amounts comparable to the amount that would be extracted by this process, in a sense the sea-borne uranium becomes a sustainable resource.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_economics">Fission economics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fission economics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economics_of_new_nuclear_power_plants" title="Economics of new nuclear power plants">Economics of new nuclear power plants</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="612" data-file-width="1095" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/300px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/450px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg/600px-Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fukushima_I_by_Digital_Globe_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, the second worst <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_incident" title="Nuclear incident">nuclear incident</a>, displaced 50,000 households after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactive material</a> leaked into the air, soil and sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Radiation checks led to bans on some shipments of vegetables and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The economics of new nuclear power plants is a controversial subject, since there are diverging views on this topic, and multibillion-dollar investments ride on the choice of an energy source. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">Nuclear power plants</a> typically have high capital costs for building the plant, but low direct fuel costs.</p>
<p>In recent years there has been a slowdown of electricity demand growth and financing has become more difficult, which affects large projects such as nuclear reactors, with very large upfront costs and long project cycles which carry a large variety of risks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kidd2011_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-61">[61]</a></sup> In Eastern Europe, a number of long-established projects are struggling to find finance, notably Belene in Bulgaria and the additional reactors at Cernavoda in Romania, and some potential backers have pulled out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kidd2011_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-61">[61]</a></sup> Where cheap gas is available and its future supply relatively secure, this also poses a major problem for nuclear projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kidd2011_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-kidd2011-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="1030" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/300px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/450px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png/600px-Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_%28Ipsos_2011%29.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_public_support_for_energy_sources_(Ipsos_2011).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Two months after the Fukushima<span class="nowrap"> </span>I failures, a global public support survey by <a href="/wiki/Ipsos" title="Ipsos">Ipsos</a> (2011) for energy sources was published and nuclear/fission was found to be the least popular<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Analysis of the economics of nuclear power must take into account who bears the risks of future uncertainties. To date all operating nuclear power plants were developed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalized" title="Nationalized">state-owned</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regulated_market" title="Regulated market">regulated</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_utility" title="Electric utility">utility monopolies</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ft-20100912_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ft-20100912-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NERA-20120316_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NERA-20120316-64">[64]</a></sup> where many of the risks associated with construction costs, operating performance, fuel price, and other factors were borne by consumers rather than suppliers. Many countries have now liberalized the <a href="/wiki/Electricity_market" title="Electricity market">electricity market</a> where these risks, and the risk of cheaper competitors emerging before capital costs are recovered, are borne by plant suppliers and operators rather than consumers, which leads to a significantly different evaluation of the economics of new nuclear power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MIT-2003_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-2003-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two of the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Pressurized_Reactor" title="European Pressurized Reactor">EPRs</a> under construction (in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and France) are significantly behind schedule and substantially over cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patel_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patel-66">[66]</a></sup> Following the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, costs are likely to go up for currently operating and new nuclear power plants, due to increased requirements for on-site spent fuel management and elevated design basis threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2011_xv_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology_2011_xv-67">[67]</a></sup> While first of their kind designs, such as the EPRs under construction are behind schedule and over-budget, of the seven South Korean <a href="/wiki/APR-1400" title="APR-1400">APR-1400s</a> presently under construction worldwide, two are in S.Korea at the <a href="/wiki/Hanul_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Hanul Nuclear Power Plant">Hanul Nuclear Power Plant</a> and four are at the largest nuclear station construction project in the world as of 2016, in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> at the planned <a href="/wiki/Barakah_nuclear_power_plant" title="Barakah nuclear power plant">Barakah nuclear power plant</a>. The first reactor, Barakah-1 is 85% completed and on schedule for grid-connection during 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_sources">Renewable sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Renewable sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy commercialization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alternative_Energies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="2982" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/300px-Alternative_Energies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/450px-Alternative_Energies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alternative_Energies.jpg/600px-Alternative_Energies.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alternative_Energies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">sun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a> are three renewable energy sources.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> is generally defined as energy that comes from resources which are naturally replenished on a human timescale such as <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">waves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal heat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Renewable energy replaces conventional fuels in four distinct areas: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_hot_water" title="Solar hot water">hot water</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Motor_fuel" title="Motor fuel">motor fuels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">rural (off-grid)</a> energy services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren15_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren15-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 16% of global final energy consumption presently comes from <a href="/wiki/Renewable_resource" title="Renewable resource">renewable resources</a>, with 10% <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> of all energy from traditional <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>, mainly used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heating" title="Heating">heating</a>, and 3.4% from <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectricity</a>. New renewables (small hydro, modern biomass, wind, solar, geothermal, and biofuels) account for another 3% and are growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ria21_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ria21-73">[73]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of energy supply. National renewable energy markets are projected to continue to grow strongly in the coming decade and beyond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a>, for example, is growing at the rate of 30% annually, with a worldwide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Installed_wind_power_capacity" title="Installed wind power capacity">installed capacity</a> of 282,482 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a> (MW) at the end of 2012.</p>
<p>Renewable energy resources exist over wide geographical areas, in contrast to other energy sources, which are concentrated in a limited number of countries. Rapid deployment of renewable energy and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a> is resulting in significant <a href="/wiki/Energy_security_and_renewable_technology" title="Energy security and renewable technology">energy security</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">climate change mitigation</a>, and economic benefits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In international public opinion surveys there is strong support for promoting renewable sources such as solar power and wind power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNEP_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNEP-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>While many renewable energy projects are large-scale, renewable technologies are also suited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> and remote areas and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_developing_countries" title="Renewable energy in developing countries">developing countries</a>, where energy is often crucial in <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(humanity)" title="Human development (humanity)">human development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' Secretary-General <a href="/wiki/Ban_Ki-moon" title="Ban Ki-moon">Ban Ki-moon</a> has said that renewable energy has the ability to lift the poorest nations to new levels of prosperity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renewableenergyworld1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-renewableenergyworld1-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hydroelectricity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1768" data-file-width="2847" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/220px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/330px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg/440px-ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ThreeGorgesDam-China2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 22,500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a> <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in China – the <a href="/wiki/List_of_conventional_hydroelectric_power_stations#Hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of conventional hydroelectric power stations">world's largest</a> hydroelectric power station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> is electric power generated by <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>; the force of falling or flowing water. In 2015 hydropower generated 16.6% of the world's total electricity and 70% of all renewable electricity <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and is expected to increase about 3.1% each year for the next 25 years.</p>
<p>Hydropower is produced in 150 countries, with the Asia-Pacific region generating 32 percent of global hydropower in 2010. China is the largest hydroelectricity producer, with 721 terawatt-hours of production in 2010, representing around 17 percent of domestic electricity use. There are now three hydroelectricity plants larger than 10 GW: the <a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> in China, <a href="/wiki/Itaipu_Dam" title="Itaipu Dam">Itaipu Dam</a> across the Brazil/Paraguay border, and <a href="/wiki/Guri_Dam" title="Guri Dam">Guri Dam</a> in Venezuela.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi2012_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cost of hydroelectricity is relatively low, making it a competitive source of renewable electricity. The average cost of electricity from a hydro plant larger than 10 megawatts is 3 to 5 U.S. cents per kilowatt-hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi2012_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-80">[80]</a></sup> Hydro is also a flexible source of electricity since plants can be ramped up and down very quickly to adapt to changing energy demands. However, damming interrupts the flow of rivers and can harm local ecosystems, and building large dams and reservoirs often involves displacing people and wildlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wi2012_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-wi2012-80">[80]</a></sup> Once a hydroelectric complex is constructed, the project produces no direct waste, and has a considerably lower output level of the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> than <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> powered energy plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-REN21-2011_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-REN21-2011-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind">Wind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Wind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:220px;max-width:220px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="472" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg/218px-Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg/327px-Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg/436px-Pretty_flamingos_-_geograph.org.uk_-_578705.jpg 2x" width="218"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Burbo_Bank_Offshore_Wind_Farm" title="Burbo Bank Offshore Wind Farm">Burbo Bank Offshore Wind Farm</a> in Northwest England</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:220px;max-width:220px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg/218px-Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg/327px-Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg/436px-Global_Wind_Power_Cumulative_Capacity.svg.png 2x" width="218"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_by_country" title="Wind power by country">Global growth</a> of wind power capacity</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">Wind power</a> harnesses the power of the wind to propel the blades of <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>. These turbines cause the rotation of <a href="/wiki/Magnet" title="Magnet">magnets</a>, which creates electricity. Wind towers are usually built together on <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>. There are <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">offshore</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_onshore_wind_farms" title="List of onshore wind farms">onshore</a> wind farms. <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_by_country" title="Wind power by country">Global wind power capacity</a> has expanded rapidly to 336 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigawatt" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> in June 2014, and wind energy production was around 4% of total worldwide electricity usage, and growing rapidly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea2014-halfyear_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwea2014-halfyear-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind power is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_European_Union" title="Wind power in the European Union">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_China" title="Wind power in China">Asia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glob_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glob-83">[83]</a></sup> Several countries have achieved relatively high levels of wind power penetration, such as 21% of stationary electricity production in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Denmark" title="Wind power in Denmark">Denmark</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-84">[84]</a></sup> 18% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Portugal" title="Wind power in Portugal">Portugal</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-84">[84]</a></sup> 16% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Spain" title="Wind power in Spain">Spain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-84">[84]</a></sup> 14% in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Ireland" title="Wind power in Ireland">Ireland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> and 9% in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Germany" title="Wind power in Germany">Germany</a> in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wwea_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-wwea-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup> By 2011, at times over 50% of electricity in Germany and Spain came from wind and solar power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> As of 2011, 83 countries around the world are using wind power on a commercial basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup></p>
<p>Many of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_onshore_wind_farms#Largest_operational_onshore_wind_farms" title="List of onshore wind farms">world's largest onshore wind farms</a> are located in the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_China" title="Wind power in China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_India" title="Wind power in India">India</a>. Most of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">world's largest offshore wind farms</a> are located in <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Denmark" title="Wind power in Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Germany" title="Wind power in Germany">Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Wind power in the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. The two largest offshore wind farm are currently the 630 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">MW</a> <a href="/wiki/London_Array" title="London Array">London Array</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gwynt_y_M%C3%B4r" title="Gwynt y Môr">Gwynt y Môr</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar">Solar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Solar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_PV_systems" title="Solar PV systems">Solar PV systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">Concentrated solar power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Plant_kl.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="679" data-file-width="1008" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/225px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/338px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Solar_Plant_kl.jpg/450px-Solar_Plant_kl.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>

</div>
