</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">Solar energy</a>, radiant <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">sun</a>, is harnessed using a range of ever-evolving technologies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_heating" title="Solar heating">solar heating</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaics" title="Solar photovoltaics">solar photovoltaics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_electricity" title="Solar thermal electricity">solar thermal electricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_architecture" title="Solar architecture">solar architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Solar technologies are broadly characterized as either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passive_solar" title="Passive solar">passive solar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Active_solar" title="Active solar">active solar</a> depending on the way they capture, convert and distribute solar energy. Active solar techniques include the use of photovoltaic panels and <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal</a> collectors to harness the energy. Passive solar techniques include orienting a building to the Sun, selecting materials with favorable <a href="/wiki/Thermal_mass" title="Thermal mass">thermal mass</a> or light dispersing properties, and designing spaces that <a href="/wiki/Ventilation_(architecture)" title="Ventilation (architecture)">naturally circulate air</a>.</p>
<p>In 2011, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> said that "the development of affordable, inexhaustible and clean solar energy technologies will have huge longer-term benefits. It will increase countries’ energy security through reliance on an indigenous, inexhaustible and mostly <a href="/wiki/Energy_independence" title="Energy independence">import-independent resource</a>, enhance <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, reduce pollution, lower the costs of mitigating <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, and keep <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> prices lower than otherwise. These advantages are global. Hence the additional costs of the incentives for early deployment should be considered learning investments; they must be wisely spent and need to be widely shared".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ie11_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-96">[96]</a></sup> More than 100 countries use solar PV.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_(8159037280).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg/220px-Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg/330px-Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg/440px-Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_%288159037280%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_Panels_at_Topaz_Solar_6_(8159037280).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a> is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations#World.27s_largest_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">world's largest solar power stations</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">Photovoltaics</a> (PV) is a method of <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generating electrical power</a> by converting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a> into <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> using <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a> that exhibit the <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_effect" title="Photovoltaic effect">photovoltaic effect</a>. Photovoltaic power generation employs <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> composed of a number of <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> containing a photovoltaic material. Materials presently used for photovoltaics include <a href="/wiki/Monocrystalline_silicon" title="Monocrystalline silicon">monocrystalline silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polycrystalline_silicon" title="Polycrystalline silicon">polycrystalline silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon" title="Amorphous silicon">amorphous silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride" title="Cadmium telluride">cadmium telluride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper_indium_gallium_selenide" title="Copper indium gallium selenide">copper indium gallium selenide</a>/sulfide. Due to the increased demand for <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources, the manufacturing of solar cells and <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system" title="Photovoltaic system">photovoltaic arrays</a> has advanced considerably in recent years.</p>
<p>Solar photovoltaics is a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable</a> energy source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> By the end of 2011, a total of 71.1 GW<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-2013_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-epia-2013-99">[99]</a></sup> had been installed, sufficient to generate 85 TWh/year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia2012_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-epia2012-100">[100]</a></sup> And by end of 2012, the 100 GW installed capacity milestone was achieved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Solar photovoltaics is now, after hydro and wind power, the third most important renewable energy source in terms of globally installed capacity. In 2016, after another year of rapid growth, solar generated 1.3% of global power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Driven by advances in technology and increases in manufacturing scale and sophistication, the cost of photovoltaics has declined steadily since the first solar cells were manufactured,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> and the levelised cost of electricity (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LCOE" title="LCOE">LCOE</a>) from PV is competitive with conventional electricity sources in an expanding list of geographic regions. <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">Net metering</a> and financial incentives, such as preferential <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariffs</a> for solar-generated electricity, have supported solar PV installations in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon#Energy_payback_time" title="Crystalline silicon">Energy Payback Time</a> (EPBT), also known as <i>energy amortization</i>, depends on the location's annual solar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> and temperature profile, as well as on the used type of PV-technology. For conventional <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> photovoltaics, the EPBT is higher than for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thin_film_solar_cell" title="Thin film solar cell">thin-film technologies</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cadmium_telluride_photovoltaics" title="Cadmium telluride photovoltaics">CdTe-PV</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics" title="Concentrated photovoltaics">CPV</a>-systems. Moreover, the payback time decreased in the recent years due to a number of improvements such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell_efficiency" title="Solar cell efficiency">solar cell efficiency</a> and more economic manufacturing processes. As of 2014, photovoltaics recoup on average the energy needed to manufacture them in 0.7 to 2 years. This results in about 95% of net-clean energy produced by a solar rooftop PV system over a 30-year life-time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup> Installations may be ground-mounted (and sometimes integrated with farming and grazing) or built into the roof or walls of a building (either <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaics" title="Building-integrated photovoltaics">building-integrated photovoltaics</a> or simply rooftop).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biofuels">Biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_biofuel" title="Sustainable biofuel">Sustainable biofuel</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:229px;max-width:229px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="500" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/225px-Soybeanbus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/338px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/450px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A bus fueled by <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EthanolPetrol.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="800" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/225px-EthanolPetrol.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/338px-EthanolPetrol.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/EthanolPetrol.jpg/450px-EthanolPetrol.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Information on pump regarding <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> blend up to 10%, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A biofuel is a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> that contains energy from geologically recent <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>. These fuels are produced from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_organisms" title="Living organisms">living organisms</a>. Examples of this <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a> occur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae">microalgae</a>. These fuels are made by a <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> conversion (biomass refers to recently living organisms, most often referring to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a> or plant-derived materials). This biomass can be converted to convenient energy containing substances in three different ways: thermal conversion, chemical conversion, and biochemical conversion. This biomass conversion can result in fuel in <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> form. This new biomass can be used for biofuels. Biofuels have increased in popularity because of rising <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_prices" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> and the need for <a href="/wiki/Energy_security" title="Energy security">energy security</a>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">Bioethanol</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fermentation" title="Ethanol fermentation">fermentation</a>, mostly from <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> produced in <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulosic biomass</a>, derived from non-food sources, such as trees and grasses, is also being developed as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedstock" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a> for ethanol production. Ethanol can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_additive" title="Fuel additive">additive</a> to increase octane and improve vehicle emissions. Bioethanol is widely used in the <a href="/wiki/Biofuel_in_the_United_States" title="Biofuel in the United States">USA</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel_in_Brazil" title="Ethanol fuel in Brazil">Brazil</a>. Current plant design does not provide for converting the <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a> portion of plant raw materials to fuel components by fermentation.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is made from <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oils</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_fat" title="Animal fat">animal fats</a>. Biodiesel can be used as a fuel for vehicles in its pure form, but it is usually used as a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> additive to reduce levels of particulates, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> from diesel-powered vehicles. Biodiesel is produced from oils or fats using <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> and is the most common biofuel in Europe. However, research is underway on producing renewable fuels from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_fuels_by_decarboxylation" title="Renewable fuels by decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, worldwide biofuel production reached 105 billion liters (28 billion gallons US), up 17% from 2009,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-107">[107]</a></sup> and biofuels provided 2.7% of the world's fuels for road transport, a contribution largely made up of ethanol and biodiesel.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Global <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> production reached 86 billion liters (23 billion gallons US) in 2010, with the United States and Brazil as the world's top producers, accounting together for 90% of global production. The world's largest biodiesel producer is the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, accounting for 53% of all biodiesel production in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biofuels2010_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Biofuels2010-107">[107]</a></sup> As of 2011, mandates for blending biofuels exist in 31 countries at the national level and in 29 states or provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ren212011_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:13–14</sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> has a goal for biofuels to meet more than a quarter of world demand for transportation fuels by 2050 to reduce dependence on petroleum and coal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geothermal">Geothermal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geothermal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2400" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/220px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/330px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg/440px-NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NesjavellirPowerPlant_edit2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Steam rising from the <a href="/wiki/Nesjavellir_Geothermal_Power_Station" title="Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station">Nesjavellir Geothermal Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Geothermal energy is <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> generated and stored in the Earth. Thermal energy is the energy that determines the <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> of matter. The geothermal energy of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> originates from the original formation of the planet (20%) and from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of minerals (80%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ucsusa_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-ucsusa-109">[109]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">geothermal gradient</a>, which is the difference in temperature between the core of the planet and its surface, drives a continuous conduction of thermal energy in the form of <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> from the core to the surface. The adjective <i>geothermal</i> originates from the Greek roots <i>γη (ge)</i>, meaning earth, and <i>θερμος (thermos)</i>, meaning hot.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">Earth's internal heat</a> is thermal energy generated from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> and continual heat loss from Earth's formation. Temperatures at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_mantle_boundary" title="Core mantle boundary">core-mantle boundary</a> may reach over 4000 °C (7,200 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The high temperature and pressure in Earth's interior cause some rock to melt and solid <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> to behave plastically, resulting in portions of <a href="/wiki/Mantle_convection" title="Mantle convection">mantle convecting</a> upward since it is lighter than the surrounding rock. Rock and water is heated in the crust, sometimes up to 370 °C (700 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>From <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_springs" title="Hot springs">hot springs</a>, geothermal energy has been used for bathing since <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> times and for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_heating" title="Space heating">space heating</a> since ancient Roman times, but it is now better known for <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity generation</a>. Worldwide, 11,400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatts" title="Megawatts">megawatts</a> (MW) of geothermal power is online in 24 countries in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> An additional 28 gigawatts of direct <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_heating" title="Geothermal heating">geothermal heating</a> capacity is installed for district heating, space heating, spas, industrial processes, desalination and agricultural applications in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geothermal power is cost effective, reliable, sustainable, and environmentally friendly,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> but has historically been limited to areas near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate_boundaries" title="Tectonic plate boundaries">tectonic plate boundaries</a>. Recent technological advances have dramatically expanded the range and size of viable resources, especially for applications such as home heating, opening a potential for widespread exploitation. Geothermal wells release greenhouse gases trapped deep within the earth, but these emissions are much lower per energy unit than those of fossil fuels. As a result, geothermal power has the potential to help mitigate <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> if widely deployed in place of fossil fuels.</p>
<p>The Earth's geothermal resources are theoretically more than adequate to supply humanity's energy needs, but only a very small fraction may be profitably exploited. Drilling and exploration for deep resources is very expensive. Forecasts for the future of geothermal power depend on assumptions about technology, energy prices, subsidies, and interest rates. Pilot programs like EWEB's customer opt in Green Power Program <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> show that customers would be willing to pay a little more for a renewable energy source like geothermal. But as a result of government assisted research and industry experience, the cost of generating geothermal power has decreased by 25% over the past two decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> In 2001, geothermal energy cost between two and ten US cents per kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic">Oceanic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Oceanic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marine_energy" title="Marine energy">Marine energy</a></div>
<p><b>Marine energy</b> or <b>marine power</b> (also sometimes referred to as <b>ocean energy</b>, <b>ocean power</b>, or <b>marine and hydrokinetic energy</b>) refers to the energy carried by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_wave" title="Ocean wave">ocean waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_thermal_energy" title="Ocean thermal energy">ocean temperature differences</a>. The movement of water in the world's oceans creates a vast store of <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, or energy in motion. This energy can be harnessed to <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">generate</a> electricity to power homes, transport and industries.</p>
<p>The term marine energy encompasses both <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave power</a> i.e. power from surface waves, and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a> i.e. obtained from the kinetic energy of large bodies of moving water. <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">Offshore wind power</a> is not a form of marine energy, as wind power is derived from the wind, even if the <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> are placed over water. The oceans have a tremendous amount of energy and are close to many if not most concentrated populations. Ocean energy has the potential of providing a substantial amount of new <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> around the world.</p>
<h3><span id="100%_renewable_energy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="100.25_renewable_energy">100% renewable energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 100% renewable energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a></div>
<p>The incentive to use 100% renewable energy, for electricity, transport, or even total primary energy supply globally, has been motivated by <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> and other ecological as well as economic concerns. <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">Renewable energy use</a> has grown much faster than anyone anticipated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pg11_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-pg11-118">[118]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> has said that there are few fundamental technological limits to integrating a portfolio of renewable energy technologies to meet most of total global energy demand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_2011_17_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC_2011_17-119">[119]</a></sup> At the national level, at least 30 nations around the world already have renewable energy contributing more than 20% of energy supply. Also, Professors S. Pacala and <a href="/wiki/Robert_H._Socolow" title="Robert H. Socolow">Robert H. Socolow</a> have developed a series of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stabilization_wedges" title="Stabilization wedges">stabilization wedges</a>" that can allow us to maintain our quality of life while avoiding catastrophic climate change, and "renewable energy sources," in aggregate, constitute the largest number of their "wedges." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pacala_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pacala-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">Mark Z. Jacobson</a> says producing all new energy with <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> by 2030 is feasible and existing energy supply arrangements could be replaced by 2050. Barriers to implementing the renewable energy plan are seen to be "primarily social and political, not technological or economic". Jacobson says that energy costs with a wind, solar, water system should be similar to today's energy costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enpol2011_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-enpol2011-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly, in the United States, the independent National Research Council has noted that "sufficient domestic renewable resources exist to allow renewable electricity to play a significant role in future electricity generation and thus help confront issues related to climate change, energy security, and the escalation of energy costs … Renewable energy is an attractive option because renewable resources available in the United States, taken collectively, can supply significantly greater amounts of electricity than the total current or projected domestic demand." .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Critics of the "100% renewable energy" approach include <a href="/wiki/Vaclav_Smil" title="Vaclav Smil">Vaclav Smil</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_E._Hansen" title="James E. Hansen">James E. Hansen</a>. Smil and Hansen are concerned about the <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable output</a> of solar and wind power, but <a href="/wiki/Amory_Lovins" title="Amory Lovins">Amory Lovins</a> argues that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_grid" title="Electricity grid">electricity grid</a> can cope, just as it routinely backs up nonworking coal-fired and nuclear plants with working ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lovi12_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-lovi12-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Google spent $30 million on their RE&lt;C project to develop renewable energy and stave off catastrophic climate change. The project was cancelled after concluding that a best-case scenario for rapid advances in renewable energy could only result in emissions 55 percent below the fossil fuel projections for 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_energy_efficiency">Increased energy efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Increased energy efficiency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="962" data-file-width="873" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/220px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/330px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg/440px-Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Compact-Fluorescent-Bulb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A spiral-type integrated <a href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lamp" title="Compact fluorescent lamp">compact fluorescent lamp</a>, which has been popular among North American consumers since its introduction in the mid-1990s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">Efficient energy use</a></div>
<p>Although increasing the efficiency of energy use is not energy development per se, it may be considered under the topic of energy development since it makes existing energy sources available to do work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:22</sup></p>
<p>Efficient energy use reduces the amount of energy required to provide products and services. For example, <a href="/wiki/Building_insulation" title="Building insulation">insulating a home</a> allows a building to use less heating and cooling energy to maintain a comfortable temperature. Installing <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_lamp" title="Fluorescent lamp">fluorescent lamps</a> or natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skylight_(window)" title="Skylight (window)">skylights</a> reduces the amount of energy required for illumination compared to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulbs" title="Incandescent light bulbs">incandescent light bulbs</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compact_fluorescent_lights" title="Compact fluorescent lights">Compact fluorescent lights</a> use two-thirds less energy and may last 6 to 10 times longer than incandescent lights. Improvements in energy efficiency are most often achieved by adopting an efficient technology or production process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reducing energy use may save consumers money, if the energy savings offsets the cost of an energy efficient technology. Reducing energy use reduces emissions. According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>, improved energy efficiency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_efficient_buildings" title="Energy efficient buildings">buildings</a>, industrial processes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sustainable_transportation" title="Sustainable transportation">transportation</a> could reduce the world's energy needs in 2050 by one third, and help control global emissions of greenhouse gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>Energy efficiency and <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> are said to be the <i>twin pillars</i> of sustainable energy policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In many countries energy efficiency is also seen to have a national security benefit because it can be used to reduce the level of energy imports from foreign countries and may slow down the rate at which domestic energy resources are depleted.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Transmission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pipeline-small_image,_seen_from_below.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="330" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Pipeline-small_image%2C_seen_from_below.jpeg/220px-Pipeline-small_image%2C_seen_from_below.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Pipeline-small_image%2C_seen_from_below.jpeg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pipeline-small_image,_seen_from_below.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An elevated section of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Alaska_Pipeline_System" title="Trans-Alaska Pipeline System">Alaska Pipeline</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While new sources of energy are only rarely discovered or made possible by new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technologies" title="Technologies">technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distribution_(business)" title="Distribution (business)">distribution</a> technology continually evolves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a> in cars, for example, is an anticipated delivery technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> This section presents the various delivery technologies that have been important to historic energy development. They all rely in way on the energy sources listed in the previous section.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping_and_pipelines">Shipping and pipelines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Shipping and pipelines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">Pipeline transport</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and their derivatives are delivered by boat, <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail</a>, or road. Petroleum and natural gas may also be delivered by <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline</a>, and coal via a <a href="/wiki/Slurry_pipeline" title="Slurry pipeline">Slurry pipeline</a>. Fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquified_petroleum_gas" title="Liquified petroleum gas">LPG</a> may also be delivered via <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a>. Natural gas pipelines must maintain a certain minimum pressure to function correctly. The higher costs of ethanol transportation and storage are often prohibitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wired_energy_transfer">Wired energy transfer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Wired energy transfer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electricalgrid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="582" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Electricalgrid.jpg/220px-Electricalgrid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Electricalgrid.jpg/330px-Electricalgrid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Electricalgrid.jpg/440px-Electricalgrid.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electricalgrid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">Electrical grid</a> – pylons and cables distribute power</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">Electrical grid</a></div>
<p>Electricity grids are the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_network" title="Electrical network">networks</a> used to <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">transmit</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electricity_distribution" title="Electricity distribution">distribute</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">power</a> from production source to end user, when the two may be hundreds of kilometres away. Sources include electrical generation plants such as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>, coal burning power plant, etc. A combination of sub-stations and transmission lines are used to maintain a constant flow of electricity. Grids may suffer from transient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_blackout" title="Power blackout">blackouts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">brownouts</a>, often due to weather damage. During certain extreme <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a> events <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> can interfere with transmissions. Grids also have a predefined <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> or load that cannot safely be exceeded. When power requirements exceed what's available, failures are inevitable. To prevent problems, power is then rationed.</p>
<p>Industrialised countries such as Canada, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a>, and Australia are among the highest per capita consumers of electricity in the world, which is possible thanks to a widespread electrical distribution network. The US grid is one of the most advanced, although <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> maintenance is becoming a problem. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20041014214845/http://currentenergy.lbl.gov/" rel="nofollow">CurrentEnergy</a> provides a realtime overview of the electricity supply and demand for <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, and the Northeast of the US. African countries with small scale electrical grids have a correspondingly low annual per capita usage of electricity. One of the most powerful power grids in the world supplies power to the state of <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>, Australia.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wireless_energy_transfer">Wireless energy transfer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Wireless energy transfer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wireless_power_transfer" title="Wireless power transfer">Wireless power transfer</a> is a process whereby electrical energy is transmitted from a power source to an electrical load that does not have a built-in power source, without the use of interconnecting wires. Currently available technology is limited to short distances and relatively low power level.</p>
<p>Orbiting solar power collectors would require wireless transmission of power to Earth. The proposed method involves creating a large beam of microwave-frequency radio waves, which would be aimed at a collector antenna site on the Earth. Formidable technical challenges exist to ensure the safety and profitability of such a scheme.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Storage">Storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stwlan.dam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="750" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/220px-Stwlan.dam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/330px-Stwlan.dam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Stwlan.dam.jpg/440px-Stwlan.dam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stwlan.dam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Ffestiniog_Power_Station" title="Ffestiniog Power Station">Ffestiniog Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, United Kingdom. <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">Pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a> (PSH) is used for <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">grid energy storage</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">Energy storage</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_energy_storage_projects" title="List of energy storage projects">List of energy storage projects</a></div>
<p>Energy storage is accomplished by devices or physical media that store <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> to perform useful operation at a later time. A device that stores energy is sometimes called an <a href="/wiki/Accumulator_(energy)" title="Accumulator (energy)">accumulator</a>.</p>
<p>All forms of energy are either <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">Chemical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">electrical energy</a>, temperature differential, <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a>, etc.) or <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>). Some technologies provide only short-term energy storage, and others can be very long-term such as <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a> using <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">storage of heat or cold between opposing seasons</a> in deep aquifers or bedrock. A wind-up clock stores potential energy (in this case mechanical, in the spring tension), a <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">battery</a> stores readily convertible chemical energy to operate a mobile phone, and a <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric</a> dam stores <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_power_industry" title="Electrical power industry">energy</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Reservoir" title="Reservoir">reservoir</a> as gravitational <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy_storage#Air_conditioning" title="Thermal energy storage">Ice storage</a> tanks store ice (<a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> in the form of latent heat) at night to meet peak demand for cooling. <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">Fossil fuels</a> such as coal and gasoline store ancient energy derived from sunlight by organisms that later died, became buried and over time were then converted into these fuels. Even <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> (which is made by the same process as fossil fuels) is a form of energy stored in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> form.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1199" data-file-width="903" height="292" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG/220px-Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG/330px-Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG/440px-Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doel_Kerncentrale.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Energy generators past and present at <a href="/wiki/Doel" title="Doel">Doel</a>, Belgium: 17th-century windmill <i>Scheldemolen</i> and 20th-century <a href="/wiki/Doel_Nuclear_Power_Station" title="Doel Nuclear Power Station">Doel Nuclear Power Station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since prehistory, when humanity discovered fire to warm up and roast food, through the Middle Ages in which populations built windmills to grind the wheat, until the modern era in which nations can get electricity splitting the atom. Man has sought endlessly for energy sources.</p>
<p>Except nuclear, geothermal and <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal</a>, all other energy sources are from current solar isolation or from fossil remains of plant and animal life that relied upon sunlight. Ultimately, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar energy</a> itself is the result of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>'s nuclear fusion. <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">Geothermal power</a> from hot, hardened <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> above the <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> of the Earth's core is the result of the decay of radioactive materials present beneath the Earth's crust, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> relies on man-made fission of heavy radioactive elements in the Earth's crust; in both cases these elements were produced in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions before the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a>.</p>
<p>Since the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the question of the future of energy supplies has been of interest. In 1865, <a href="/wiki/William_Stanley_Jevons" title="William Stanley Jevons">William Stanley Jevons</a> published <i>The Coal Question</i> in which he saw that the reserves of coal were being depleted and that oil was an ineffective replacement. In 1914, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bureau_of_Mines" title="United States Bureau of Mines">U.S. Bureau of Mines</a> stated that the total production was 5.7 billion barrels (910,000,000 m<sup>3</sup>). In 1956, Geophysicist <a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a> deduces that U.S. oil production would <a href="/wiki/Hubbert_peak_theory" title="Hubbert peak theory">peak</a> between 1965 and 1970 and that oil production will peak "within half a century" on the basis of 1956 data. In 1989, predicted peak by <a href="/wiki/Colin_Campbell_(geologist)" title="Colin Campbell (geologist)">Colin Campbell</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In 2004, OPEC estimated, with substantial investments, it would nearly double oil output by 2025<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Energy-consumption-World2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1427" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Energy-consumption-World2.png/400px-Energy-consumption-World2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Energy-consumption-World2.png/600px-Energy-consumption-World2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Energy-consumption-World2.png/800px-Energy-consumption-World2.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Energy-consumption-World2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Energy consumption from 1989 to 1999</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">Climate change mitigation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_pricing" title="Carbon pricing">Carbon pricing</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental movement</a> has emphasized <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a> of energy use and development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> is sustainable in its production; the available supply will not be diminished for the foreseeable future - millions or billions of years. "Sustainability" also refers to the ability of the environment to cope with waste products, especially <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>. Sources which have no direct waste products (such as wind, solar, and hydropower) are brought up on this point. With global demand for energy growing, the need to adopt various energy sources is growing. <a href="/wiki/Energy_conservation" title="Energy conservation">Energy conservation</a> is an alternative or complementary process to energy development. It reduces the demand for energy by using it efficiently.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resilience">Resilience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Resilience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Energy_per_capita.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="800" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Energy_per_capita.png/400px-Energy_per_capita.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Energy_per_capita.png/600px-Energy_per_capita.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Energy_per_capita.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Energy_per_capita.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Energy consumption <i>per capita</i> (2001). Red hues indicate increase, green hues decrease of consumption during the 1990s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some observers contend that idea of "energy independence" is an unrealistic and opaque concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The alternative offer of "energy resilience" is a goal aligned with economic, security, and energy realities. The notion of resilience in energy was detailed in the 1982 book <i><a href="/wiki/Brittle_Power" title="Brittle Power">Brittle Power</a>: Energy Strategy for National Security</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The authors argued that simply switching to domestic energy would not be secure inherently because the true weakness is the interdependent and vulnerable energy infrastructure of the United States. Key aspects such as gas lines and the electrical power grid are centralized and easily susceptible to disruption. They conclude that a "resilient energy supply" is necessary for both national security and the environment. They recommend a focus on energy efficiency and renewable energy that is decentralized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intel_Corporation" title="Intel Corporation">Intel Corporation</a> Chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Grove" title="Andrew Grove">Andrew Grove</a> looked to energy resilience, arguing that complete independence is unfeasible given the global market for energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> He describes energy resilience as the ability to adjust to interruptions in the supply of energy. To that end, he suggests the U.S. make greater use of electricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Electricity can be produced from a variety of sources. A diverse energy supply will be less affected by the disruption in supply of any one source. He reasons that another feature of electrification is that electricity is "sticky" – meaning the electricity produced in the U.S. is to stay there because it cannot be transported overseas. According to Grove, a key aspect of advancing electrification and energy resilience will be converting the U.S. automotive fleet from gasoline-powered to electric-powered. This, in turn, will require the modernization and expansion of the electrical power grid. As organizations such as <a href="/wiki/The_Reform_Institute" title="The Reform Institute">The Reform Institute</a> have pointed out, advancements associated with the developing <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">smart grid</a> would facilitate the ability of the grid to absorb vehicles <i>en masse</i> connecting to it to charge their batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Present_and_future"><span id="Future"></span> Present and future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Energy_development&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Present and future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption_outlook.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1078" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/World_energy_consumption_outlook.png/400px-World_energy_consumption_outlook.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/World_energy_consumption_outlook.png/600px-World_energy_consumption_outlook.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/World_energy_consumption_outlook.png/800px-World_energy_consumption_outlook.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption_outlook.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Outlook—World Energy Consumption by Fuel (as of 2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#2589ba; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a href="/wiki/Liquid_fuel" title="Liquid fuel">Liquid fuels</a> incl. <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">Biofuels</a> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#b57537; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#5f943a; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Gas" title="Natural Gas">Natural Gas</a><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#8d3742; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renewable_fuel" title="Renewable fuel">Renewable fuels</a> <span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#87732c; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fuel" title="Nuclear fuel">Nuclear fuels</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png/400px-World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png/600px-World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png/800px-World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption_by_region_1970-2025.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Increasing share of energy consumption by developing nations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#4747bf; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialized_nation" title="Industrialized nation">Industrialized nations</a><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#0e7a0d; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nation" title="Developing nation">Developing nations</a><br/>
<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:none; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#730774; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;"> </span> </span> <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">EE</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Former_Soviet_Union" title="Former Soviet Union">Former Soviet Union</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Extrapolations from current knowledge to the future offer a choice of energy futures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Predictions parallel the <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a> hypothesis. Numerous are complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_modeling" title="Scientific modeling">models</a> based <a href="/wiki/Scenario" title="Scenario">scenarios</a> as pioneered by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limits_to_Growth" title="Limits to Growth">Limits to Growth</a></i>. Modeling approaches offer ways to analyze diverse <a href="/wiki/Strategy" title="Strategy">strategies</a>, and hopefully find a road to rapid and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> of humanity. Short term <a href="/wiki/Energy_crisis" title="Energy crisis">energy crises</a> are also a concern of energy development. Extrapolations lack plausibility, particularly when they predict a continual increase in oil consumption.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Energy production usually requires an energy investment. Drilling for oil or building a wind power plant requires energy. The fossil fuel resources that are left are often increasingly difficult to extract and convert. They may thus require increasingly higher energy investments. If investment is greater than the value of the energy produced by the resource, it is no longer an effective energy source. These resources are no longer an energy source but may be exploited for value as raw materials. New technology may lower the energy investment required to extract and convert the resources, although ultimately basic physics sets limits that cannot be exceeded.</p>
<p>Between 1950 and 1984, as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> transformed <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> around the globe, world grain production increased by 250%. The energy for the Green Revolution was provided by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilizers" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a> (natural gas), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> (oil), and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> fueled <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The peaking of world hydrocarbon production (<a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>) may lead to significant changes, and require sustainable methods of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> One vision of a sustainable energy future involves all human structures on the earth's surface (i.e., buildings, vehicles and roads) doing <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a> (using sunlight to split water as a source of hydrogen and absorbing carbon dioxide to make fertilizer) efficiently than plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>With contemporary <a href="/wiki/Space_industry" title="Space industry">space industry</a>'s economic activity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bromberg2000-1_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bromberg2000-1-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schrogl2010_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schrogl2010-149">[149]</a></sup> and the related <a href="/wiki/Private_spaceflight" title="Private spaceflight">private spaceflight</a>, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manufacturing_industries" title="Manufacturing industries">manufacturing industries</a>, that go into Earth's orbit or beyond, delivering them to those regions will require further energy development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Researchers have contemplated <a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">space-based solar power</a> for collecting solar power for use on Earth. Space-based solar power has been in research since the early 1970s. Space-based solar power would require construction of collector structures in space. The advantage over ground-based solar power is higher intensity of light, and no weather to interrupt power collection.</p>
